         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">
          <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_spectral_types" title="Asteroid spectral types">
           Spectral type
          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/S-type_asteroid" title="S-type asteroid">
          S
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilsonKeilLove1999p479_10-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-WilsonKeilLove1999p479-10">
           [10]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">
          <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_(H)" title="Absolute magnitude">
           Absolute magnitude
           <i>
            (H)
           </i>
          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         9.94
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL2008_3-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-JPL2008-3">
           [3]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        243 Ida
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">
            aɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ) is an
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroid
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Koronis_family" title="Koronis family">
        Koronis family
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
        asteroid belt
       </a>
       . It was discovered on 29 September 1884, by Austrian astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Johann_Palisa" title="Johann Palisa">
        Johann Palisa
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Observatory" title="Vienna Observatory">
        Vienna Observatory
       </a>
       and named after a
       <a href="/wiki/Nymph" title="Nymph">
        nymph
       </a>
       from Greek mythology. Later telescopic observations categorized Ida as an
       <a href="/wiki/S-type_asteroid" title="S-type asteroid">
        S-type asteroid
       </a>
       , the most numerous type in the inner asteroid belt. On 28 August 1993, Ida was visited by the unmanned
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft, while en route to
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       . It was the second asteroid visited by a spacecraft and the first found to have a
       <a href="/wiki/Minor-planet_moon" title="Minor-planet moon">
        natural satellite
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Like all main-belt asteroids, Ida's orbit lies between the planets
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       and Jupiter. Its
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">
        orbital period
       </a>
       is 4.84 years, and its
       <a href="/wiki/Rotation_period" title="Rotation period">
        rotation period
       </a>
       is 4.63 hours. Ida has an average diameter of 31.4 km (19.5 mi). It is irregularly shaped and elongated, and apparently composed of two large objects connected together. Its surface is one of the most heavily cratered in the Solar System, featuring a wide variety of crater sizes and ages.
      </p>
      <p>
       Ida's moon,
       <a href="#Moon">
        Dactyl
       </a>
       , was discovered by mission member Ann Harch in images returned from
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       . It was named after the
       <a href="/wiki/Dactyl_(mythology)" title="Dactyl (mythology)">
        Dactyls
       </a>
       , creatures which inhabited Mount Ida in Greek mythology. Dactyl, being only 1.4 kilometres (4,600 ft) in diameter, is about one-twentieth the size of Ida. Its orbit around Ida could not be determined with much accuracy. However, the constraints of possible orbits allowed a rough determination of Ida's density, which revealed that it is depleted of metallic
       <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">
        minerals
       </a>
       . Dactyl and Ida share many characteristics, suggesting a common origin.
      </p>
      <p>
       The images returned from
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       , and the subsequent measurement of Ida's mass, provided new insights into the geology of S-type asteroids. Before the
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       flyby, many different theories had been proposed to explain their mineral composition. Determining their composition permits a correlation between
       <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">
        meteorites
       </a>
       falling to the Earth and their origin in the asteroid belt. Data returned from the flyby pointed to S-type asteroids as the source for the
       <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_chondrite" title="Ordinary chondrite">
        ordinary chondrite
       </a>
       meteorites, the most common type found on the Earth's surface.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>
