      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_observations">
        Discovery and observations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery and observations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Ida was discovered on 29 September 1884 by
       <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
        Austrian
       </a>
       astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Johann_Palisa" title="Johann Palisa">
        Johann Palisa
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Observatory" title="Vienna Observatory">
        Vienna Observatory
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ridpath1897p206_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ridpath1897p206-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was his 45th asteroid discovery.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raab2002_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Raab2002-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida was
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_minor_planets#Numbering_and_naming_conventions" title="List of minor planets">
        named
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Moriz_von_Kuffner" title="Moriz von Kuffner">
        Moriz von Kuffner
       </a>
       , a Viennese brewer and amateur astronomer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmadel2003p36_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schmadel2003p36-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger2003p241_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Berger2003p241-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
        Greek mythology
       </a>
       , Ida was a
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ida_(Crete)" title="Mount Ida (Crete)">
        nymph
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">
        Crete
       </a>
       who raised the god
       <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">
        Zeus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA2005_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA2005-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida was recognized as a member of the
       <a href="/wiki/Koronis_family" title="Koronis family">
        Koronis family
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Kiyotsugu_Hirayama" title="Kiyotsugu Hirayama">
        Kiyotsugu Hirayama
       </a>
       , who proposed in 1918 that the group comprised the remnants of a destroyed precursor body.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p700_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p700-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ida's
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy#Planets,_asteroids,_and_comets" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">
        reflection spectrum
       </a>
       was measured on 16 September 1980 by astronomers David J. Tholen and Edward F. Tedesco as part of the eight-color asteroid survey (ECAS).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZellnerTholenTedesco1985p357p373_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ZellnerTholenTedesco1985p357p373-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its spectrum matched those of the asteroids in the S-type classification.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZellnerTholenTedesco1985p410_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ZellnerTholenTedesco1985p410-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many observations of Ida were made in early 1993 by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory_Flagstaff_Station" title="United States Naval Observatory Flagstaff Station">
        US Naval Observatory in Flagstaff
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_Observatory" title="Oak Ridge Observatory">
        Oak Ridge Observatory
       </a>
       . These improved the measurement of Ida's orbit around the Sun and reduced the uncertainty of its position during the
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       flyby from 78 to 60 km (48 to 37 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OwenYeomans1994p2295_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OwenYeomans1994p2295-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">
        Exploration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Exploration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_trajectory_Ida.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="332" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Galileo_trajectory_Ida.svg/220px-Galileo_trajectory_Ida.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Galileo_trajectory_Ida.svg/330px-Galileo_trajectory_Ida.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Galileo_trajectory_Ida.svg/440px-Galileo_trajectory_Ida.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_trajectory_Ida.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Trajectory of
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         from launch to Jupiter orbital insertion
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Galileo_flyby">
        <i>
         Galileo
        </i>
        flyby
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Galileo flyby">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Ida was visited in 1993 by the
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       -bound space probe
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       . Its encounters of the asteroids
       <a href="/wiki/951_Gaspra" title="951 Gaspra">
        Gaspra
       </a>
       and Ida were secondary to the Jupiter mission. These were selected as targets in response to a new NASA policy directing mission planners to consider asteroid flybys for all spacecraft crossing the belt.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p26_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p26-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       No prior missions had attempted such a flyby.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p699_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p699-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       was launched into orbit by the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">
        Space Shuttle
        <i>
         Atlantis
        </i>
       </a>
       mission
       <a href="/wiki/STS-34" title="STS-34">
        STS-34
       </a>
       on 18 October 1989.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p24_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p24-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Changing
       <i>
        Galileo's
       </i>
       trajectory to approach Ida required that it consume 34 kg (75 lb) of
       <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">
        propellant
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p72_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p72-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mission planners delayed the decision to attempt a flyby until they were certain that this would leave the spacecraft enough propellant to complete its Jupiter mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p36_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p36-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ida-approach.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="293" data-file-width="399" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Ida-approach.gif/220px-Ida-approach.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Ida-approach.gif/330px-Ida-approach.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/15/Ida-approach.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ida-approach.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Images from the flyby, starting 5.4 hours before closest approach and showing Ida's rotation
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Galileo's
       </i>
       trajectory carried it into the asteroid belt twice on its way to Jupiter. During its second crossing, it flew by Ida on 28 August 1993 at a speed of 12,400 m/s (41,000 ft/s) relative to the asteroid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p36_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-D'AmarioBrightWolf1992p36-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The onboard imager observed Ida from a distance of 240,350 km (149,350 mi) to its closest approach of 2,390 km (1,490 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA2005_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA2005-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p120_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p120-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida was the second asteroid, after Gaspra, to be imaged by a spacecraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen1993p215_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cowen1993p215-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       About 95% of Ida's surface came into view of the probe during the flyby.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomasBeltonCarcichChapman1996_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ThomasBeltonCarcichChapman1996-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Transmission of many Ida images was delayed due to a permanent failure in the spacecraft's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">
        high-gain antenna
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1994p358_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1994p358-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first five images were received in September 1993.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These comprised a high-resolution
       <a href="/wiki/Image_stitching" title="Image stitching">
        mosaic
       </a>
       of the asteroid at a resolution of 31–38 m/
       <a href="/wiki/Pixel" title="Pixel">
        pixel
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChapmanBeltonVeverkaNeukum1994p237_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ChapmanBeltonVeverkaNeukum1994p237-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreeleySullivanPappalardoVeverka1994p469_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreeleySullivanPappalardoVeverka1994p469-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The remaining images were sent in February 1994,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holm1994_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holm1994-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       when the spacecraft's proximity to the Earth allowed higher speed transmissions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MonetStoneMonetDahn1994p2293_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MonetStoneMonetDahn1994p2293-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discoveries">
        Discoveries
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Discoveries">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The data returned from the
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       flybys of Gaspra and Ida, and the later
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">
         NEAR Shoemaker
        </a>
       </i>
       asteroid mission, permitted the first study of asteroid
       <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
        geology
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p57_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p57-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida's relatively large surface exhibited a diverse range of geological features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChapmanBeltonVeverkaNeukum1994p238_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ChapmanBeltonVeverkaNeukum1994p238-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The discovery of Ida's moon
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dactyl_(moon)#Moon" title="Dactyl (moon)">
        Dactyl
       </a>
       , the first confirmed satellite of an asteroid, provided additional insights into Ida's composition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p709_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p709-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ida is classified as an
       <a href="/wiki/S-type_asteroid" title="S-type asteroid">
        S-type asteroid
       </a>
       based on ground-based
       <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">
        spectroscopic measurements
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ByrnesD'Amario1994_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ByrnesD'Amario1994-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The composition of S-types was uncertain before the
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       flybys, but was interpreted to be either of two minerals found in meteorites that had fallen to the Earth:
       <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_chondrite" title="Ordinary chondrite">
        ordinary chondrite
       </a>
       (OC) and
       <a href="/wiki/Meteorite#Meteorite_types" title="Meteorite">
        stony-iron
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilsonKeilLove1999p479_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-WilsonKeilLove1999p479-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Estimates of Ida's density are constrained to less than 3.2 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       by the long-term stability of Dactyl's orbit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ByrnesD'Amario1994_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ByrnesD'Amario1994-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This all but rules out a stony-iron composition; were Ida made of 5 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       iron- and nickel-rich material, it would have to contain more than 40% empty space.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p709_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p709-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Galileo images also led to the discovery that space weathering was taking place on Ida, a process which causes older regions to become more red in color over time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p700_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p700-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p710_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p710-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The same process affects both Ida and its moon, although Dactyl shows a lesser change.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1995p496_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1995p496-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The weathering of Ida's surface revealed another detail about its composition: the reflection spectra of freshly exposed parts of the surface resembled that of OC meteorites, but the older regions matched the spectra of S-type asteroids.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p699_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p699-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NWA869Meteorite.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/NWA869Meteorite.jpg/220px-NWA869Meteorite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/NWA869Meteorite.jpg/330px-NWA869Meteorite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/NWA869Meteorite.jpg/440px-NWA869Meteorite.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NWA869Meteorite.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Polished section of an
         <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_chondrite" title="Ordinary chondrite">
          ordinary chondrite
         </a>
         meteorite
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Both of these discoveries—the space weathering effects and the low density—led to a new understanding about the relationship between S-type asteroids and OC meteorites. S-types are the most numerous kind of asteroid in the inner part of the asteroid belt.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p699_21-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p699-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       OC meteorites are, likewise, the most common type of meteorite found on the Earth's surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p699_21-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p699-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The reflection spectra measured by remote observations of S-type asteroids, however, did not match that of OC meteorites. The
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       flyby of Ida found that some S-types, particularly the Koronis family, could be the source of these meteorites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1995p496_37-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1995p496-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">
        Physical characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_size_comparison.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1056" data-file-width="1348" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Asteroid_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Asteroid_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Asteroid_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Asteroid_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Asteroid_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Asteroid_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Size comparison of Ida, several other asteroids, the dwarf planet Ceres, and Mars
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ida's mass is between 3.65 and 4.99 × 10
       <sup>
        16
       </sup>
       kg.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p179t180_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p179t180-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">
        gravitational field
       </a>
       produces an acceleration of about 0.3 to 1.1 cm/s
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       over its surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomasBeltonCarcichChapman1996_7-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ThomasBeltonCarcichChapman1996-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This field is so weak that an astronaut standing on its surface could leap from one end of Ida to the other, and an object moving in excess of 20 m/s (70 ft/s) could
       <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">
        escape
       </a>
       the asteroid entirely.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeisslerPetitDurdaGreenberg1996p142_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GeisslerPetitDurdaGreenberg1996p142-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p99_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p99-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:243_Ida_rotation.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="915" data-file-width="1563" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/243_Ida_rotation.jpg/220px-243_Ida_rotation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/243_Ida_rotation.jpg/330px-243_Ida_rotation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/243_Ida_rotation.jpg/440px-243_Ida_rotation.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:243_Ida_rotation.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Successive images of a rotating Ida
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ida is a distinctly elongated asteroid,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p58_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p58-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with an irregular surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1994p363_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1994p363-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BottkeCellinoPaolicchiBinzel2002p10_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BottkeCellinoPaolicchiBinzel2002p10-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida is 2.35 times as long as it is wide,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p58_41-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p58-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a "waist" separates it into two geologically dissimilar halves.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This constricted shape is consistent with Ida being made of two large, solid components, with loose
       <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">
        debris
       </a>
       filling the gap between them. However, no such debris was seen in high-resolution images captured by
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BottkeCellinoPaolicchiBinzel2002p10_43-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-BottkeCellinoPaolicchiBinzel2002p10-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although there are a few steep slopes tilting up to about 50° on Ida, the slope generally does not exceed 35°.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomasBeltonCarcichChapman1996_7-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-ThomasBeltonCarcichChapman1996-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida's irregular shape is responsible for the asteroid's very uneven gravitational field.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen1995_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cowen1995-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The surface acceleration is lowest at the extremities because of their high rotational speed. It is also low near the "waist" because the mass of the asteroid is concentrated in the two halves, away from this location.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomasBeltonCarcichChapman1996_7-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-ThomasBeltonCarcichChapman1996-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_features">
        Surface features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Surface features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_243_Ida_and_Dactyl" title="List of geological features on 243 Ida and Dactyl">
        List of geological features on 243 Ida and Dactyl
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:243_Ida_large.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1229" data-file-width="1973" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/243_Ida_large.jpg/220px-243_Ida_large.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/243_Ida_large.jpg/330px-243_Ida_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/243_Ida_large.jpg/440px-243_Ida_large.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:243_Ida_large.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Mosaic of images recorded by
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         3.5 minutes before its closest approach
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ida's surface appears heavily
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        cratered
       </a>
       and mostly gray, although minor color variations mark newly formed or uncovered areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA2005_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA2005-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Besides craters, other features are evident, such as grooves, ridges, and protrusions. Ida is covered by a thick layer of
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       , loose debris that obscures the solid rock beneath. The largest, boulder-sized, debris fragments are called
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ejecta_block&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ejecta block (page does not exist)">
         ejecta blocks
        </a>
       </i>
       , several of which have been observed on the surface.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Regolith">
        Regolith
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Regolith">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The surface of Ida is covered in a blanket of pulverized rock, called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
         regolith
        </a>
       </i>
       , about 50–100 m (160–330 ft) thick.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This material is produced in
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">
        impact events
       </a>
       and redistributed across Ida's surface by geological processes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p96_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p96-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       observed evidence of recent
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_wasting" title="Mass wasting">
        downslope regolith movement
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreeleySullivanPappalardoVeverka1994p470_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreeleySullivanPappalardoVeverka1994p470-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ida's regolith is composed of the
       <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
        silicate
       </a>
       minerals
       <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">
        olivine
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">
        pyroxene
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holm1994_2-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holm1994-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p701_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p701-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its appearance changes over time through a process called
       <a href="/wiki/Space_weathering" title="Space weathering">
        space weathering
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1995p496_37-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1995p496-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because of this process, older regolith appears more red in color compared to freshly exposed material.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p710_36-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p710-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:218px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ejecta_block_on_243_Ida.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="216" data-file-width="216" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ejecta_block_on_243_Ida.svg/216px-Ejecta_block_on_243_Ida.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ejecta_block_on_243_Ida.svg/324px-Ejecta_block_on_243_Ida.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ejecta_block_on_243_Ida.svg/432px-Ejecta_block_on_243_Ida.svg.png 2x" width="216"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ejecta_block_on_243_Ida.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         image of a 150 m (490 ft) block at 24.8°S, 2.8°E
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p90_48-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p90-48">
           [48]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       About 20 large (40–150 m across) ejecta blocks have been identified, embedded in Ida's regolith.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeisslerPetitDurdaGreenberg1996p141_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GeisslerPetitDurdaGreenberg1996p141-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ejecta blocks constitute the largest pieces of the regolith.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p132_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p132-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because ejecta blocks are expected to break down quickly by impact events, those present on the surface must have been either formed recently or uncovered by an impact event.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen1995_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cowen1995-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p97_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p97-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most of them are located within the craters Lascaux and Mammoth, but they may not have been produced there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p97_51-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p97-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This area attracts debris due to Ida's irregular gravitational field.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen1995_44-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cowen1995-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some blocks may have been ejected from the young crater Azzurra on the opposite side of the asteroid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stooke1997p1385_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stooke1997p1385-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Structures">
        Structures
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Structures">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Several major structures mark Ida's surface. The asteroid appears to be split into two halves, here referred to as
       <i>
        region 1
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        region 2
       </i>
       , connected by a "waist".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This feature may have been filled in by debris, or blasted out of the asteroid by impacts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stooke1997p1385_52-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stooke1997p1385-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Region 1 of Ida contains two major structures. One is a prominent 40 km (25 mi) ridge named
       <i>
        Townsend Dorsum
       </i>
       that stretches 150 degrees around Ida's surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SárneczkyKereszturi2002_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SárneczkyKereszturi2002-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The other structure is a large indentation named
       <i>
        Vienna Regio
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ida's region 2 features several sets of grooves, most of which are 100 m (330 ft) wide or less and up to 4 km (2.5 mi) long.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p131_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p131-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are located near, but are not connected with, the craters Mammoth, Lascaux, and Kartchner.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p132_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p132-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some grooves are related to major impact events, for example a set opposite Vienna Regio.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomasProckter2004_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ThomasProckter2004-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Craters">
        Craters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Craters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Ida is one of the most densely cratered bodies yet explored in the Solar System,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChapmanBeltonVeverkaNeukum1994p237_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ChapmanBeltonVeverkaNeukum1994p237-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1994p363_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1994p363-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and impacts have been the primary process shaping its surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Cratering has reached the saturation point, meaning that new impacts erase evidence of old ones, leaving the total crater count roughly the same.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707p708_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707p708-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is covered with craters of all sizes and stages of degradation,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1994p363_42-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1994p363-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and ranging in age from fresh to as old as Ida itself.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The oldest may have been formed during the breakup of the
       <a href="/wiki/Koronis_family" title="Koronis family">
        Koronis family
       </a>
       parent body.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1995p496_37-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1995p496-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The largest crater, Lascaux, is almost 12 km (7.5 mi) across.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BottkeCellinoPaolicchiBinzel2002p10_43-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-BottkeCellinoPaolicchiBinzel2002p10-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-USGS-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Region 2 contains nearly all of the craters larger than 6 km (3.7 mi) in diameter, but Region 1 has no large craters at all.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p707_28-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p707-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some craters are arranged in chains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreeleySullivanPappalardoVeverka1994p469_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreeleySullivanPappalardoVeverka1994p469-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fingal_on_243_Ida.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="360" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Fingal_on_243_Ida.svg/360px-Fingal_on_243_Ida.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Fingal_on_243_Ida.svg/540px-Fingal_on_243_Ida.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Fingal_on_243_Ida.svg/720px-Fingal_on_243_Ida.svg.png 2x" width="360"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fingal_on_243_Ida.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Asymmetric 1.5 km (0.93 mi) wide crater Fingal at 13.2°S, 39.9°E
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_58-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-USGS-58">
           [58]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ida's major craters are named after caves and
       <a href="/wiki/Lava_tube" title="Lava tube">
        lava tubes
       </a>
       on Earth. The crater Azzurra, for example, is named after a submerged cave on the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Capri" title="Capri">
        Capri
       </a>
       , also known as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Blue_Grotto_(Bi%C5%A1evo)" title="Blue Grotto (Biševo)">
         Blue Grotto
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreeleyBatson2001p393_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreeleyBatson2001p393-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Azzurra seems to be the most recent major impact on Ida.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeisslerPetitDurdaGreenberg1996p141_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-GeisslerPetitDurdaGreenberg1996p141-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The ejecta from this collision is distributed discontinuously over Ida
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p710_36-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p710-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and is responsible for the large-scale color and
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       variations across its surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An exception to the crater morphology is the fresh, asymmetric Fingal, which has a sharp boundary between the floor and wall on one side.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pappalardo124_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pappalardo124-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another significant crater is Afon, which marks Ida's
       <a href="/wiki/Prime_meridian" title="Prime meridian">
        prime meridian
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seidelmann_Archinal_A'hearn_et_al._2007_p._171_9-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Seidelmann_Archinal_A'hearn_et_al._2007_p._171-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The craters are simple in structure: bowl-shaped with no flat bottoms and no central peaks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pappalardo124_61-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pappalardo124-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are distributed evenly around Ida, except for a protrusion north of crater Choukoutien which is smoother and less cratered.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Ejecta" title="Ejecta">
        ejecta
       </a>
       excavated by impacts is deposited differently on Ida than on planets because of its rapid rotation, low gravity and irregular shape.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p58_41-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p58-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Ejecta_blanket" title="Ejecta blanket">
        Ejecta blankets
       </a>
       settle asymmetrically around their craters, but fast-moving ejecta that escapes from the asteroid is permanently lost.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">
        Composition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Composition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Ida was classified as an
       <a href="/wiki/S-type_asteroid" title="S-type asteroid">
        S-type asteroid
       </a>
       based on the similarity of its reflectance spectra with similar asteroids.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilsonKeilLove1999p479_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-WilsonKeilLove1999p479-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       S-types may share their composition with stony-iron or
       <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_chondrite" title="Ordinary chondrite">
        ordinary chondrite
       </a>
       (OC) meteorites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilsonKeilLove1999p479_10-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-WilsonKeilLove1999p479-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The composition of the interior has not been directly analyzed, but is assumed to be similar to OC material based on observed surface color changes and Ida's
       <a href="/wiki/Bulk_density" title="Bulk density">
        bulk density
       </a>
       of 2.27–3.10 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1995p496_37-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1995p496-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilsonKeilLove1999p480_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-WilsonKeilLove1999p480-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       OC meteorites contain varying amounts of the silicates
       <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">
        olivine
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">
        pyroxene
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        iron
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">
        feldspar
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis1996p89_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lewis1996p89-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Olivine and pyroxene were detected on Ida by
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holm1994_2-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holm1994-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The mineral content appears to be homogeneous throughout its extent.
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       found minimal variations on the surface, and the asteroid's spin indicates a consistent density.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomasProckter2004p21_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ThomasProckter2004p21-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p135_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p135-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Assuming that its composition is similar to OC meteorites, which range in density from 3.48 to 3.64 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       , Ida would have a
       <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">
        porosity
       </a>
       of 11–42%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilsonKeilLove1999p480_64-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-WilsonKeilLove1999p480-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ida's interior probably contains some amount of impact-fractured rock, called
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaregolith" title="Megaregolith">
         megaregolith
        </a>
       </i>
       . The megaregolith layer of Ida extends between hundreds of meters below the surface to a few kilometers. Some rock in Ida's core may have been fractured below the large craters Mammoth, Lascaux, and Undara.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p135_67-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SullivanGreeleyPappalardoAsphaug1996p135-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">
        Orbit and rotation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Orbit and rotation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ida_orbit.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="216" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ida_orbit.svg/220px-Ida_orbit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ida_orbit.svg/330px-Ida_orbit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ida_orbit.svg/440px-Ida_orbit.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ida_orbit.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Orbit and positions of Ida and five planets as of 9 March 2009
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ida is a member of the
       <a href="/wiki/Koronis_family" title="Koronis family">
        Koronis family
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
        asteroid-belt
       </a>
       asteroids.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p700_15-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p700-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida orbits the Sun at an average distance of 2.862 AU (428.1 Gm), between the orbits of
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holm1994_2-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holm1994-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL2008_3-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-JPL2008-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida takes 4.84089 years to complete one orbit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL2008_3-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-JPL2008-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ida's
       <a href="/wiki/Rotation_period" title="Rotation period">
        rotation period
       </a>
       is 4.63 hours,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VokrouhlickyNesvornyBottke2003p147_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VokrouhlickyNesvornyBottke2003p147-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p58_41-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-GeisslerPetitGreenberg1996p58-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       making it one of the fastest rotating asteroids yet discovered.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p107_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p107-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The calculated maximum
       <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia" title="Moment of inertia">
        moment of inertia
       </a>
       of a uniformly dense object the same shape as Ida coincides with the spin axis of the asteroid. This suggests that there are no major variations of density within the asteroid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomasProckter2004_55-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ThomasProckter2004-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida's axis of rotation
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precession_(astronomy)" title="Precession (astronomy)">
        precesses
       </a>
       with a period of 77 thousand years, due to the gravity of the Sun acting upon the nonspherical shape of the asteroid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slivan1995p134_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Slivan1995p134-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">
        Origin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Origin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Ida originated in the breakup of the roughly 120 km (75 mi) diameter Koronis parent body.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VokrouhlickyNesvornyBottke2003p147_8-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-VokrouhlickyNesvornyBottke2003p147-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The progenitor asteroid had partially differentiated, with heavier metals migrating to the core.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p117_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p117-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ida carried away insignificant amounts of this core material.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p117_70-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p117-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is uncertain how long ago the disruption event occurred. According to an analysis of Ida's cratering processes, its surface is more than a billion years old.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p117_70-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p117-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, this is inconsistent with the estimated age of the Ida–Dactyl system of less than 100 million years;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HurfordGreenberg2000p1595_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HurfordGreenberg2000p1595-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it is unlikely that Dactyl, due to its small size, could have escaped being destroyed in a major collision for longer. The difference in age estimates may be explained by an increased rate of cratering from the debris of the Koronis parent body's destruction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarrollOstlie1996p878_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CarrollOstlie1996p878-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Moon">
        Moon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Moon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dactyl1.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="350" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Dactyl1.jpg/220px-Dactyl1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Dactyl1.jpg/330px-Dactyl1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/Dactyl1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dactyl1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Highest-resolution image of Dactyl, recorded while
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         was about 3,900 km away from the moon
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ida has a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" title="Asteroid moon">
        moon
       </a>
       named Dactyl, official designation
       <b>
        (243) Ida I Dactyl
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          DAK
         </span>
         -til
        </i>
       </a>
       ). It was discovered in images taken by the
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       spacecraft during its flyby in 1993. These images provided the first direct confirmation of an asteroid moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p709_34-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p709-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the time, it was separated from Ida by a distance of 90 kilometres (56 mi), moving in a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prograde_orbit" title="Prograde orbit">
        prograde orbit
       </a>
       . Dactyl is heavily cratered, like Ida, and consists of similar materials. Its origin is uncertain, but evidence from the flyby suggests that it originated as a fragment of the Koronis parent body.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">
        Discovery
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Discovery">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Dactyl was found on 17 February 1994 by
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       mission member Ann Harch, while examining delayed image downloads from the spacecraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holm1994_2-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holm1994-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       recorded 47 images of Dactyl over an observation period of 5.5 hours in August 1993.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p177_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p177-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The spacecraft was 10,760 kilometres (6,690 mi) from Ida
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeltonCarlson1994_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BeltonCarlson1994-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 10,870 kilometres (6,750 mi) from Dactyl when the first image of the moon was captured, 14 minutes before
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       made its closest approach.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mason1994p108_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mason1994p108-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Dactyl was initially
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_naming_conventions#Natural_satellites_of_planets" title="Astronomical naming conventions">
        designated
       </a>
       1993 (243) 1.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeltonCarlson1994_74-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-BeltonCarlson1994-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green1994_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Green1994-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was named by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        International Astronomical Union
       </a>
       in 1994,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green1994_76-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Green1994-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       for the mythological
       <a href="/wiki/Dactyl_(mythology)" title="Dactyl (mythology)">
        dactyls
       </a>
       who inhabited
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Ida,_Crete" title="Mount Ida, Crete">
        Mount Ida
       </a>
       on the island of Crete.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmadel2003p37_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schmadel2003p37-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pausanias576_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pausanias576-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics_2">
        Physical characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Dactyl is an "egg-shaped"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p709_34-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p709-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but "remarkably spherical"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmadel2003p37_77-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schmadel2003p37-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       object measuring 1.6 by 1.4 by 1.2 kilometres (0.99 mi × 0.87 mi × 0.75 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p709_34-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p709-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was oriented with its longest axis pointing towards Ida.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p709_34-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p709-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Like Ida, Dactyl's surface exhibits saturation cratering.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p709_34-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p709-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is marked by more than a dozen craters with a diameter greater than 80 m (260 ft), indicating that the moon has suffered many collisions during its history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA2005_14-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA2005-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At least six craters form a linear chain, suggesting that it was caused by locally produced debris, possibly ejected from Ida.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996p709_34-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996p709-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Dactyl's craters may contain central peaks, unlike those found on Ida.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsphaugRyanZuber2003p463_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AsphaugRyanZuber2003p463-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These features, and Dactyl's
       <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">
        spheroidal
       </a>
       shape, imply that the moon is gravitationally controlled despite its small size.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsphaugRyanZuber2003p463_79-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-AsphaugRyanZuber2003p463-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Like Ida, its average temperature is about 200 K (−73 °C; −100 °F).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holm1994_2-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holm1994-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Dactyl shares many characteristics with Ida. Their
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedos
       </a>
       and reflection
       <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">
        spectra
       </a>
       are very similar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChapmanKlaasenBeltonVeverka1994p455_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ChapmanKlaasenBeltonVeverka1994p455-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The small differences indicate that the space weathering process is less active on Dactyl.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1995p496_37-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1995p496-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its small size would make the formation of significant amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       impossible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1995p496_37-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1995p496-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeltonCarlson1994_74-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-BeltonCarlson1994-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This contrasts with Ida, which is covered by a deep layer of regolith.
      </p>
      <p>
       The two largest imaged craters on Dactyl were named Acmon and Celmis, after two of the mythological dactyls. Acmon is the largest crater in the above image, and Celmis is near the bottom, mostly obscured in shadow. The craters are 300 and 200 meters in diameter, respectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dactylnamedfeatures_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dactylnamedfeatures-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">
        Orbit
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Orbit">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dactyl_potential_orbits.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="794" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Dactyl_potential_orbits.svg/220px-Dactyl_potential_orbits.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Dactyl_potential_orbits.svg/330px-Dactyl_potential_orbits.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Dactyl_potential_orbits.svg/440px-Dactyl_potential_orbits.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dactyl_potential_orbits.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram of potential orbits of Dactyl around Ida
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Dactyl's orbit around Ida is not precisely known.
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       was in the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">
        plane
       </a>
       of Dactyl's orbit when most of the images were taken, which made determining its exact orbit difficult.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ByrnesD'Amario1994_35-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ByrnesD'Amario1994-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Dactyl orbits in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_motion" title="Direct motion">
        prograde
       </a>
       direction
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p179_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p179-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and is inclined about 8° to Ida's equator.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p177_73-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p177-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Based on computer simulations, Dactyl's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericenter" title="Pericenter">
        pericenter
       </a>
       must be more than about 65 km (40 mi) from Ida for it to remain in a stable orbit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p195_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p195-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The range of orbits generated by the simulations was narrowed down by the necessity of having the orbits pass through points at which
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       observed Dactyl to be at 16:52:05 UT on 28 August 1993, about 90 km (56 mi) from Ida at longitude 85°.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p188_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p188-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p193_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p193-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 26 April 1994, the
       <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
        Hubble Space Telescope
       </a>
       observed Ida for eight hours and was unable to spot Dactyl. It would have been able to observe it if it were more than about 700 km (430 mi) from Ida.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ByrnesD'Amario1994_35-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-ByrnesD'Amario1994-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       If in a circular orbit at the distance at which it was seen, Dactyl's orbital period would be about 20 hours.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChapmanKlaasenBeltonVeverka1994p455_80-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ChapmanKlaasenBeltonVeverka1994p455-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its orbital speed is roughly 10 m/s (33 ft/s), "about the speed of a fast run or a slowly thrown baseball".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ByrnesD'Amario1994_35-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-ByrnesD'Amario1994-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Age_and_origin">
        Age and origin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=243_Ida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Age and origin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Dactyl may have originated at the same time as Ida,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p116_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p116-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from the disruption of the Koronis parent body.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p97_51-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeeVeverkaThomasHelfenstein1996p97-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, it may have formed more recently, perhaps as ejecta from a large impact on Ida.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p182_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PetitDurdaGreenbergHurford1997p182-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is extremely unlikely that it was captured by Ida.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mason1994p108_75-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mason1994p108-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Dactyl may have suffered a major impact around 100 million years ago, which reduced its size.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p117_70-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-GreenbergBottkeNolanGeissler1996p117-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

        <li id="cite_note-Cowen1993p215-26">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Cowen1993p215_26-0">
            ^
           </a>

         </span>

          </a>
          , p. 215
         </span>
         <blockquote class="templatequote">
          <p>
           <span class="reference-text">
            Nearly a month after a successful photo session, the Galileo spacecraft last week finished radioing to Earth a high-resolution portrait of the second asteroid ever to be imaged from space. Known as 243 Ida, the asteroid was photographed from an average distance of just 3,400 kilometers some 3.5 minutes before Galileo's closest approach on Aug. 28.
           </span>
          </p>

        <li id="cite_note-62">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

            ^

        <li id="cite_note-63">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-63">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="#CITEREFGeisslerPetitDurdaGreenberg1996">
           Geissler et al. 1996
          </a>
          , p. 155
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-WilsonKeilLove1999p480-64">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          ^
          <a href="#cite_ref-WilsonKeilLove1999p480_64-0">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              a
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
          <a href="#cite_ref-WilsonKeilLove1999p480_64-1">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              b
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="#CITEREFWilsonKeilLove1999">
           Wilson, Keil &amp; Love 1999
          </a>
          , p. 480
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-Lewis1996p89-65">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Lewis1996p89_65-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="#CITEREFLewis1996">
           Lewis 1996
          </a>
          , p. 89
         </span>
         <blockquote class="templatequote">
          <p>
           <span class="reference-text">
            The chondrites fall naturally into five composition classes, of which three have very similar mineral contents, but different proportions of metal and silicates. All three contain abundant iron in three different forms (ferrous iron oxide in silicates, metallic iron, and ferrous sulfide), usually with all three abundant enough to be classified as potential ores. All three contain feldspar (an aluminosilicate of calcium, sodium, and potassium), pyroxene (silicates with one silicon atom for each atom of magnesium, iron, or calcium), olivine (silicates with two iron or magnesium atoms per silicon atom), metallic iron, and iron sulfide (the mineral
            <a href="/wiki/Troilite" title="Troilite">
             troilite
            </a>
            ). These three classes, referred to collectively as the ordinary chondrites, contain quite different amounts of metal.
           </span>
          </p>
         </blockquote>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ThomasProckter2004p21-66">
